      <p align="center" class="MsoNormal">
       <b>
        <span style="font-size:18.0pt;color:white">
         <br/>
        </span>
        <span style="font-size:16pt;color:white">
         ...the Ongoing Controversy over Weighting by
         <br/>
         Party 
			ID
        </span>

   <br/>
   <span class="auto-style4">
    <strong>
     <a href="http://pos.org/2015/04/a-long-horizon-some-observations-about-party-identification-over-the-past-25-years/" target="_blank">
      Party ID Numbers from the NBC-Wall Street Journal Poll, 1990-2015
     </a>
    </strong>
   </span>
   <strong>
    <br class="auto-style4"/>
   </strong>
   <br/>
   <hr/>
   <strong>
    <span class="auto-style3">
     <br/>
     <a name="summary 14">
     </a>
     <br/>
     2014 Notes
    </span>
   </strong>
   <br/>
   <br/>
   <strong>
    <span class="auto-style4">
     <em>
      New York Times
     </em>
     's exit-poll-based 
	"
     <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2014/11/04/us/politics/2014-exit-polls.html" target="_blank">
      Portrait 
	of the Electorate
     </a>
     " indicates that self-identified
     <span class="style14">
      Republicans comprised 37%
     </span>
     of the 2014 midterm electorate,
     <span class="style57">
      Democrats 36%
     </span>
     , and
     <span class="auto-style7">
      Independents 28%
     </span>
     <br/>
    </span>
    <span class="style18">
     (
     <em>
      be sure to click on "Size bars according 
	to population of groups"
     </em>
     )
     <br/>
    </span>
    <span class="auto-style4">
     <br/>
     Columbia University statistician Andrew 
	Gelman and colleagues write that:
     <br/>
     <br/>
     <table cellpadding="3" cellspacing="4" class="auto-style5" style="width: 75%">
      <tr>
       <td class="style60" style="width: 756px">
        <strong>
         <em>
          <span class="style18">
           The short story is much of the 
			apparent changes in public opinion are actuallychanges in patterns 
			of nonresponse: When it looked like [2012 GOP nominee Mitt] Romney 
			jumped in popularity, what was really happening was that disaffected 
			Democrats were not responding to the survey while resurgent 
			Republicans were more likely to respond.
           <span class="style14">
            From 
			a methods point of view, the key step is to poststratify by party 
			ID
           </span>
           ...
           <br/>
          </span>
          <span class="auto-style6">
          </span>
          <span class="style18">
           <br/>
           (
          </span>
         </em>
         <span class="style18">
          <a href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/monkey-cage/wp/2014/08/20/the-mythical-swing-voter/" target="_blank">
           <em>
            original 
			Washington Post article
           </em>
          </a>
          <em>
           and
          </em>
          <a href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/monkey-cage/wp/2014/08/21/mythical-swing-voter-update/" target="_blank">
           <em>
            addendum
           </em>
          </a>
          ; August 2014)
         </span>
        </strong>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
    </span>
   </strong>
   <br/>
   <a name="summary 12">
   </a>
   <span class="auto-style2">
    ...
    <br/>
    <span class="style10">
     ...
    </span>
    <br/>
    <table align="center" style="width: 85%">
     <tr>
      <td class="style39">
       <strong>
        Campaign 2012
       </strong>
      </td>
     </tr>
     <tr>
      <td class="style58">
       <p class="style69">
        <strong>
         <span class="style65">
          <br/>
          CNN exit polls peg
          <a href="http://www.cnn.com/election/2012/results/race/president?hpt=hp_inthenews#exit-polls" target="_blank">
           2012 Party ID breakdown
          </a>
          as
          <span class="style57">
           Democrat 38%
          </span>
          ,
          <span class="style10">
           Repub. 32%
          </span>
          , and
          <span class="style68">
           Independent 29%
          </span>
          .
         </span>
        </strong>
       </p>
       <p class="style66">
        <span class="style65">
         <strong>
          Article by Samuel Best and Brian Krueger on
          <a href="http://themonkeycage.org/blog/2012/11/07/galluping-away-from-the-herd/" target="_blank">
           Gallup missing the 2012 Party ID percentages
          </a>
          (based on exit polls) by a wide margin
         </strong>
        </span>
       </p>
       <p class="style70">
        <strong>
         Because of questionable partisan composition, Pew Research 
			Center
         <a href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2013/09/13/weight-polls-past-vote_n_3923178.html" target="_blank">
          weights its final 2012 pre-election poll
         </a>
         by who respondents say 
			they voted for in 2008 (similar to weighting by Party ID), "but 
			that's not something we like to do."
        </strong>
        <span class="style65">
         <p class="style58">
          <strong>
           <span class="style65">
            <span class="style10">
             INTRODUCTION TO CAMPAIGN 2012:
            </span>
            <font style="font-size: 14pt">
             <span class="style18">
              It's the issue that never seems to go away -- polls' partisan 
			composition (the percentages of self-identified Democrats, 
			Republicans, and Independents in the sample) and what to do (if 
			anything) when these percentages seem out of whack compared to 
			previous baselines. In August 2012 alone, four articles appeared 
			(links below), expressing different opinions on the matter. I invite 
			you to peruse these articles and all the other resources on this 
			site, which I've maintained since 2004.
             </span>
             <span class="style59">
              <br/>
              <br/>
              <table align="center" class="style61" style="width: 80%">
               <tr>
                <td class="style60">
                 <strong>
                  <a href="http://rothenbergpoliticalreport.com/news/article/handicapping-the-presidential-race-watch-the-samples-and-weighting" target="_blank">
                   <span class="style18">
                    Stu Rothenberg
                   </span>
                  </a>
                  <span class="style36">
                   suggests that "...when the partisan make-up of a sample differs significantly from the previous months, you may see a trend that says more about the samples than about the race."
                   <br/>
                   <br/>
                   <a href="http://www.realclearpolitics.com/articles/2012/08/01/cbsnytquinnipiac_swing_state_polls__party_id.html" target="_blank">
                    <span class="style16">
                     Sean Trende
                    </span>
                   </a>
                   <span class="style14">
                    notes, at least with some polls, that even when Party ID figures seem to be off, self-identified political ideology (liberal, moderate, conservative) may not be
                   </span>
                   <span class="style13">
                    .
                   </span>
                   <span class="style37">
                    <span class="style18">
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     The
                     <a href="http://www.people-press.org/2012/08/03/party-affiliation-and-election-polls/" target="_blank">
                      Pew Research Center
                     </a>
                     contends that "focusing on the partisan balance of surveys is, in almost every circumstance, the wrong place to look."
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/commentisfree/2012/aug/01/bias-polls-harry-enten" target="_blank">
                      <span class="style16">
                       Harry Enten
                      </span>
                     </a>
                     <span class="style10">
                      concurs in part, and dissents in part, with Pew. He argues that "There is one potential instance where re-weighting by party identification could be acceptable..."
                     </span>
                    </span>
                   </span>
                  </span>
                 </strong>
                </td>
               </tr>
              </table>
              <p class="style58">
               <strong>
                <font style="font-size: 14pt">
                 <span class="style36">
                  <span class="style18">
                   <span class="style37">
                    <span class="style58">
                     Over the years, weighting schemes have gotten more 
			sophisticated. Using, say, 2000 exit-poll figures on Party ID as a 
			template to weight surveys for the 2004 election indeed is very 
			questionable, as much about the political landscape can change in 
			four years (or less time). With
                    </span>
                    <span class="style10">
                     <em>
                      dynamic weighting
                     </em>
                    </span>
                    <span class="style58">
                     , a 
			pollster can implement party weights on his or her next survey based 
			on the average party ID percentages from the same pollster's, say, 
			past three months' previous surveys (
                    </span>
                   </span>
                   <span class="style37">
                    <span class="style10" font="" size="14.0pt">
                     <a href="http://www.mysterypollster.com/main/2006/05/rasmussen_adopt.html" target="_blank">
                      here
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span font="" size="14.0pt">
                     <span class="style58">
                      and
                     </span>
                     <span class="style10">
                      <a href="http://people.uncw.edu/lowery/pls302/Erikson%20Chapter%20Outlines/weighting_party_id.pdf" target="_blank">
                       here
                      </a>
                      <span font="" size="14.0pt">
                       <span class="style58">
                        ). 
			Dynamic weighting thus allows for some shifting in Party ID due to 
			recent events, but the aggregation over multiple recent polls allows 
			for greater stability in Party ID than some surveys have shown.
                        <br/>
                       </span>
                      </span>
                     </span>
                    </span>
                    <span class="style10" font="" size="14.0pt">
                     <span font="" size="14.0pt">
                      <strong>
                       <span class="style58">
                       </span>
                      </strong>
                     </span>
                    </span>
                   </span>
                  </span>
                 </span>
                </font>
               </strong>
              </p>
             </span>
            </font>
           </span>
          </strong>
         </p>
        </span>
       </p>
      </td>
     </tr>
     <tr>
      <td class="style20">
       <font style="font-size: 14pt">
        <span class="style21">
         <strong>
          Trends in Party Identification
         </strong>
        </span>
        <span class="style24">
         <strong>
          <span class="style1">
           <br class="style18"/>
          </span>
          Pollster.com/Huffington Post
         </strong>
         <a href="http://elections.huffingtonpost.com/pollster/party-identification" target="_blank">
          <strong>
           compilation
          </strong>
         </a>
         <strong>
          
         </strong>
         <a href="http://www.rasmussenreports.com/public_content/archive/mood_of_america_archive/partisan_trends/summary_of_party_affiliation" target="_blank">
          <strong>
           Rasmussen
          </strong>
         </a>
        </span>
       </font>
      </td>
     </tr>
     <tr>
      <td class="style62">
       <span class="style22">
        New "
       </span>
       <a href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-srv/politics/polls/pollmanipulator.html" target="_blank">
        Poll 
			Manipulator
       </a>
       <span class="style22">
        " Device from the
       </span>
       <em>
        <span class="style22">
         Washington Post
         <br/>
        </span>
       </em>
       You can adjust 
			the partisan split to see how the Obama-Romney horse-race numbers 
			are affected
      </td>
     </tr>
    </table>
    <strong>
     <span class="style25">
      <br/>
     </span>
     <span class="style7">
      <span class="style10">
       Other News on Party ID and Sample 
	Weighting
      </span>
      <br class="style10"/>
      <br/>
     </span>
     <span class="style33">
      After the Nov. 3
      <a href="http://firstread.nbcnews.com/_news/2012/11/02/14891866-polls-obama-stays-ahead-in-ohio-deadlocked-with-romney-in-fla" target="_blank">
       NBC/Wall St. Journal/Marist Poll
      </a>
      showed Obama up by 6 percentage points 
	in the crucial state of Ohio, NBC's Chuck Todd "tweeted" out what the result 
	would have been under an alternative sample weighting on Party ID. I applaud 
	this gesture and encourage other pollsters to report a range of results 
	based on different possible Party ID distributions.
      <br/>
      <br/>
     </span>
     <span class="style7">
      <img height="116" src="chuck%20todd%20tweet.jpg" width="548"/>
      <br/>
      <br/>
      <br/>
     </span>
     <span class="style33">
      Insightful analysis of Ohio polls by Nick Gourevitch, 
	offering a
      <a href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/nick-gourevitch/romney-lead-with-independents_b_2058290.html" target="_blank">
       simple explanation
      </a>
      for why you could have both a large Democratic edge 
	in Party ID and Romney leading among Independents (November 1, 2012)
      <br/>
      <br/>
      YouGov's Peter Kellner writes about the Romney-Obama 
	debates and Party ID weighting (or lack thereof)
      <br/>
      in the polls that 
	followed (
      <a href="http://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/peter-kellner/barack-obama-mitt-romney-us-election-_b_2005585.html" target="_blank">
       link
      </a>
      ). 
	Highly recommended! (October 24, 2012)
      <br/>
     </span>
     <span class="style7">
      <br/>
      <font color="navy" face="Arial" size="2">
       <span style="FONT-FAMILY: Arial; COLOR: navy; FONT-SIZE: 10pt">
       </span>
       <span class="style64">
        Study 
	by Cobb and Nie from the 2012 AAPOR conference (
        <a href="http://www.knowledgenetworks.com/ganp/docs/aapor2012/THE-DYNAMICS-OF-PARTISANSHIP-WITHIN-ELECTION-CYCLES-Cobb-Nie.ppt" target="_blank">
         link
        </a>
        ) 
	appears to show robust individual-level stability of partisan ID, as I read 
	it. (See especially slides 17 and 18.) The same respondents were surveyed 11 
	times, between Nov. 2007 and Dec. 2008. Most of the switching in Party ID 
	that did occur seemed to take place between strong and weak 
	identification with the same party, or from weak Democrat or Republican to 
	Independent. Almost never did a self-identified Democrat (strong or weak) 
	become a Republican at the next assessment or vice-versa. In fact, 82% of 
	respondents identified with the same party (ignoring strong or weak 
	intensity) at wave 11 as they did at wave 1. (Posted October 16, 2012.)
       </span>
      </font>
     </span>
     <span class="style2">
      <br class="style64"/>
     </span>
     <span class="style7">
      <br/>
     </span>
     <span class="style33">
      The
      <em>
       Wall Street Journal
      </em>
      's "Numbers Guy"
      <a href="http://blogs.wsj.com/numbersguy/polling-party-1173/?mod=WSJBlog&amp;utm_source=feedburner&amp;utm_medium=feed&amp;utm_campaign=Feed%3A+wsj%2Fnumbersguy%2Ffeed+%28WSJ.com%3A+The+Numbers+Guy%29" target="_blank">
       tackles the Party ID-weighting issue
      </a>
      . See the contrasting opinions of 
	political scientists John Sides and Donald Green.
      <span class="style7">
       <font color="navy" face="Arial" size="2">
        <span class="style64">
         (Posted October 16, 2012.)
        </span>
       </font>
      </span>
      <br/>
      <br/>
      Political "insiders"
      <a href="http://hotlineoncall.nationaljournal.com/archives/2012/10/insiders-weight.php" target="_blank">
       support
      </a>
      weighting by Party ID.
      <span class="style7">
       <font color="navy" face="Arial" size="2">
        <span class="style64">
         (Posted October 16, 2012.)
        </span>
       </font>
      </span>
      <br/>
     </span>
     <span class="style2">
      <br class="style18"/>
     </span>
     <span class="style63">
      <font style="font-size: 14pt">
       <font style="font-size: 12pt">
        <span class="style25">
         <span class="style33">
          Mark Blumenthal 
	offers some
          <a href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/mark-blumenthal/unskewed-polls_b_1924293.html" target="_blank">
           useful thoughts
          </a>
          on how aggregate Party ID can shift
          <br/>
          somewhat during a campaign 
	(September 28, 2012).
          <br/>
          <br/>
          <font style="font-size: 14pt">
           <font class="style18">
            Historically, it's been Democrats who have 
	complained about partisan composition being off in polls.
            <br/>
            This time
            <a href="http://nationaljournal.com/2012-presidential-campaign/republicans-to-pollsters-too-many-democrats-in-your-surveys-20120925" target="_blank">
             it's the Republicans
            </a>
            (September 25, 2012).
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Analysis of
            <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2012/09/17/1132836/-PPP-Wisc-Obama-1-but-R-4-Party-ID?showAll=yes" target="_blank">
             Wisconsin Party ID Composition
            </a>
            (September 17, 2012)
            <br/>
            <font style="font-size: 12pt">
             <span class="style10">
              Updates:
             </span>
             <a href="https://law.marquette.edu/poll/wp-content/uploads/2012/09/MLSP11_Toplines.pdf" target="_blank">
              Marquette Law School Poll
             </a>
             (Sept. 19;
             <span class="style57">
              D+8
             </span>
             , 34-26),
             <br/>
             <a href="http://www.cbsnews.com/8301-250_162-57515519/poll-obama-leads-in-virginia-wisconsin-tight-in-colorado/?pageNum=4&amp;tag=contentMain;contentBody" target="_blank">
              NY Times/CBS/Quinnipiac
             </a>
             (Sept. 19;
             <span class="style57">
              D+8
             </span>
             , 35-27)
            </font>
           </font>
          </font>
          <br/>
         </span>

                     <br/>

                     <br/>

                    </span>
                   </span>
                  </strong>
                 </td>
                 <td class="style3">
                  <strong>
                   <span class="style2">
                    <span class="style30">
                     <em>
                      11/9/2010
                     </em>
                    </span>
                   </span>
                   <span class="style27">
                    <span class="style33">
                    </span>
                   </span>
                   <span class="style2">
                    <span class="style33">
                     The
                     <em>
                      New York Times
                     </em>
                     presents its biennial 
			exit-poll-based "
                     <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2010/11/07/weekinreview/20101107-detailed-exitpolls.html" target="_blank">
                      <span class="style29">
                       Portrait 
			of the Electorate
                      </span>
                     </a>
                     ." It estimates the self-identified partisan 
			composition of the 2010 voters at 36% each Democrats and 
			Republicans, and 27% Independent... Pollster.com has a
                     <a href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2010/11/05/not-all-polls-were-wrong-_n_779705.html" target="_blank">
                      <span class="style29">
                       nice article
                      </span>
                     </a>
                    </span>
                   </span>
                   <span class="style7">
                    <span class="style25">
                     <span class="style33">
                     </span>
                     <span class="style2">
                      <span class="style33">
                       <span class="style5">
                       </span>
                      </span>
                     </span>
                    </span>
                   </span>
                   <span class="style2">
                    <span class="style33">
                     that delves into the importance of proper sample 
			weighting, not on Party ID per se, but just on basic demographics... 
			Emory University Professor Alan Abramowitz
                     <a href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/alan-abramowitz/bad-night-for-congression_b_778126.html" target="_blank">
                      <span class="style29">
                       spanks Gallup
                      </span>
                     </a>
                     for its highly inaccurate generic-ballot polling.
                    </span>
                   </span>
                   <span class="style7">
                    <span class="style29">
                     <span class="style33">
                      <br/>
                      <br/>
                     </span>
                     <em>
                      <span class="style27">
                       <span class="style2">
                        <span class="style35">
                         10/13/2010
                        </span>
                       </span>
                       <span class="style33">
                       </span>
                      </span>
                     </em>
                    </span>
                    <span class="style2">
                     <span class="style33">
                      The major trackings of Party ID seem to suggest that, heading into 
	the 2010 midterm elections,
	self-identified Democrats outnumber self-identified Republicans by a small 
	margin
	(see charts from
                      <a href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2009/06/23/party-id-rl_n_725932.html" target="_blank">
                       <span class="style29">
                        Pollster.com
                       </span>
                      </a>
                      and
                     </span>
                    </span>
                    <span class="style25">
                     <span class="style18">
                     </span>
                    </span>
                    <span class="style2">
                     <a href="http://www.rasmussenreports.com/public_content/politics/mood_of_america/partisan_trends" target="_blank">
                      <span class="style34">
                       Rasmussen
                      </span>
                     </a>
                     <span class="style25">
                      ).
                      <span class="style18">
                       Gallup's early-October likely-voter models, 
			however, are projecting a
                       <a href="http://www.dailykos.com/storyonly/2010/10/9/908765/-Why-Gallups-LV-screen-is-the-way-it-is" target="_blank">
                        <span class="style29">
                         nine-point GOP edge
                        </span>
                       </a>
                       over the Democrats in composition of the electorate;
	that would be a major departure from recent election cycles, where partisan composition has ranged from Democrat/Republican parity to Democratic advantages of several 
	percentage points (see below).
                      </span>
                     </span>
                    </span>
                   </span>
                  </strong>
                  <span class="style7">
                   <span class="style25">
                   </span>

               <p align="center" class="MsoNormal">
                <b>
                 <font color="#FF3300" size="6">
                  Pollsters' Party ID Compositions for National Surveys (2008)
                 </font>
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="center" class="MsoNormal">
                <b>
                 Rasmussen Weighting Template for Final Days Leading Up to the Election: 
	39.9% Democrat, 33.4% Republican
                </b>
                <br/>
                <strike>
                 <b>
                  <font color="#009999" size="2">
                   Rasmussen Weighting Template for Week of October 26-November 1: 40.0% 
	Democrat, 32.8% Republican
                  </font>
                 </b>
                 <font color="#009999" size="2">
                  <br/>
                 </font>
                 <b>
                  <font color="#009999">
                   <font size="2">
                    Rasmussen Weighting Template for Week of October 19-25: 39.7% 
	Democrat, 33.0% Republican
                    <br/>
                   </font>
                   <font color="#009999" size="2">
                    Rasmussen Weighting Template for Week of October 12-18: 39.3% Democrat 
	33.0% Republican
                   </font>
                  </font>
                 </b>
                 <font color="#009999">
                  <br/>
                 </font>
                </strike>
               </p>
               <p align="center" class="MsoNormal">
                <font size="4">
                 <b>
                  <a href="http://www.mydd.com/story/2008/10/10/51730/437" target="_blank">
                   Zogby Weights: 
	38% Democrat, 36% Republicans
                  </a>
                 </b>
                </font>
                <br/>
               </p>
               <p align="center" class="MsoNormal">
                <b>
                 <font size="4">
                  <a href="http://www.fivethirtyeight.com/2008/10/tracking-poll-primer.html">
                   Daily Kos/Research 2000 Weights: 35% Democrat, 26% Republican
                  </a>
                 </font>
                </b>
                <br/>
                <font color="#FF0000">
                 <i>
                  <b>
                   I had erroneously been including this poll in 
	the non-fixed-weight listing below.
                  </b>
                 </i>
                </font>
               </p>
               <p align="center" class="MsoNormal">
                <b>
                 <font size="4">
                  The polls below
                  <span class="style10">
                   do not
                  </span>
                  re-weight or adjust their results based on Party ID. Nevertheless, the 
	Party ID percentages they obtain from their random samples are informative in judging the 
	partisan composition of the electorate. Party ID percentages for a 
	given poll are shown by the respective "D" and "R" notations, with links to 
	the original sources, if still available. Obama 
	(O)-McCain (M) "horse race" numbers are shown below the Party ID 
	percentages in
                  <i>
                   italics
                  </i>
                  .
                  <br/>
                 </font>
                 <i>
                  <font color="#FF0000">
                   Daily tracking polls use a moving (or 
	rolling) average of the last few days' (usually 3) readings. For 
	example, a poll released on a
                   <br/>
                   Thursday could be an average of Monday, Tuesday, and Wednesday's polling. 
	Because polls released on adjacent days thus have some
                   <br/>
                   overlap in their data, such polls will likely show considerable stability in 
	reported results over the short term.
                   <br/>
                  </font>
                  <font color="#008000">
                   If a national poll you've seen in the news 
	is not included below, it's probably because it didn't publicly report Party 
	ID numbers.
                  </font>
                 </i>
                </b>
               </p>

               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 *Date the poll was reported.
                 <br/>
                 <sup>
                  #
                 </sup>
                 This poll measures the "horse race" question both with and 
	without the VP candidates being named. The version with the VP 
	candidates appears to be considered the primary one.
                 <br/>
                 ^For this 
	organization's 10/20 poll, adding in Independents who lean toward the parties, the Democratic number 
	would become 48 and the Republican one, 43. For the 10/24 poll, the D 
	number would become 49 and the R one, 45. For the 11/3 poll, the D 
	number would become 51 and the R one, 43.
                 <br/>
                 <sup>
                  @
                 </sup>
                 Adding in Independents who lean toward the parties, the 
	Democratic number would become 45 and the Republican one, 40.
                 <br/>
                 <sup>
                  $
                 </sup>
                 Adding in "leaners" would yield a Democratic ID of 51 and a 
	Republican one of 42 (raw numbers of D and R leaners in question 34C were 
	added to respective party totals in
                 <span style="font-family: Times New Roman">
                  PID_3WOL
                 </span>
                 )
                </b>

                 </a>
                 to return to top 
of page.
                </b>

               <hr/>
               <p align="center" class="MsoNormal">
                <b>
                 <font color="#FF0000" size="6">
                  <a name="state 08">
                  </a>
                  <br/>
                  Estimated Party ID Composition in Key 2008 States
                 </font>
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left" class="MsoNormal">
                <b>
                 October 9, 2008 -- The American 
	Research Group (ARG) released a batch of state polls today. The one 
	getting the
                 <a href="http://www.pollster.com/blogs/arg_mn_mo_mt_nh_oh_tx_wv.php">
                  most 
	discussion
                 </a>
                 is that from West Virginia, where Barack Obama is said to be 
	leading John McCain 50-42%. Though the Mountaineer State was long a 
	Democratic stronghold, the GOP has dominated at the presidential level of 
	late (Bush beat Gore
                 <a href="http://www.cnn.com/ELECTION/2000/results/WV/frameset.exclude.html">
                  by 6% in 2000
                 </a>
                 , and Kerry
                 <a href="http://www.cnn.com/ELECTION/2004/pages/results/states/WV/P/00/">
                  by 
	13% in 2004
                 </a>
                 ). Further, McCain has consistently led in West 
	Virginia during the
                 <a href="http://www.pollster.com/polls/wv/08-wv-pres-ge-mvo.php">
                  current 
	campaign
                 </a>
                 , albeit with some recent narrowing. Whenever a polling 
	result looks odd, discussion usually turns to partisan composition of the 
	sample. Did the pollster oversample Democrats or Republicans (or 
	neither)? This latest situation, involving West Virginia, has 
	motivated me to complete a project I've long been considering -- namely, 
	compiling the best estimates I can find of the Party ID composition in 
	several swing states. Below is what I've found. The chart is 
	definitely a work-in-progress, though, so please send me additional data 
	sources if you know of any (
                 <a href="mailto:alan.reifman@ttu.edu">
                  alan.reifman@ttu.edu
                 </a>
                 ).
                </b>
               </p>
               <table align="center" border="1" id="table11" width="84%">
                <tr>
                 <td align="center" bgcolor="#000000" width="137">
                  <font color="#FFFFFF" size="4">

			or Gov. race)

               <p align="left" class="MsoNormal">
                <b>
                 <u>
                  Note 1.
                 </u>
                 Apparently, these 
	pollsters used a seven-point scale, including strong, moderate/somewhat, and 
	weak Democrats and Republicans, plus "pure" independent. The notation 
	"2-3-2," I believe, thus lumps weak Democrats and Republicans with 
	Independents, and only counts strong and somewhat strong party identifiers 
	with their party; "3-1-3" would puts weak identifiers with their party.
                </b>
               </p>

                  </b>
                 </font>

                 <font color="#009999">
                  <b>
                   Are more people identifying 
	as Democrats because more people are voting for Obama? Or has party id 
	remained flat over the last two months, 
	in contrast to what we've found in our polls, and Obama is just doing better 
	because we're over sampling Democrats?
                  </b>
                 </font>

                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FFFF00" style="width: 41px; border-left: medium none; border-right: 1.0pt solid windowtext; border-top: medium none; border-bottom: 1.0pt solid windowtext; padding-left: 5.4pt; padding-right: 5.4pt; padding-top: 0in; padding-bottom: 0in" valign="top">
                   <p align="center" class="style49" style="text-align:center">
                    <font color="#808080">
                     35/37
                    </font>
                   </p>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FFFF00" style="width: 42px; border-left: medium none; border-right: 1.0pt solid windowtext; border-top: medium none; border-bottom: 1.0pt solid windowtext; padding-left: 5.4pt; padding-right: 5.4pt; padding-top: 0in; padding-bottom: 0in" valign="top">

                    </font>

                  <td align="center" style="width: 94px; border-left: medium none; border-right: 1.0pt solid windowtext; border-top: medium none; border-bottom: 1.0pt solid windowtext; padding-left: 5.4pt; padding-right: 5.4pt; padding-top: 0in; padding-bottom: 0in" valign="top">

               <p style="text-align: left">
                <font size="2">
                 <b>
                  Lettering turns
                  <font color="#808080">
                   grey
                  </font>
                  when a more recent poll by the same 
organization becomes available. All polls above are
                  <font color="#FF0000">
                   national
                  </font>
                  . For discussion of partisan composition of
                  <font color="#FF0000">
                   state
                  </font>
                  polls, see other sections of this website.
                 </b>
                </font>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 <font size="2">
                  (
                  <font color="#FF0000">
                   a
                  </font>
                  )
                  <span style="color: black">
                   I use 
data from the Rasmussen daily polls as my personal gold standard of what the 
correct partisan composition is, due to their large aggregate sample sizes and 
sophisticated methods:
                   <i>
                    
                   </i>
                  </span>
                  <i>
                   At Rasmussen Reports, we adjust 
our party identification weighting targets each month based upon actual survey 
results from the previous 90 days. For the month of
                   <font color="#008000">
                    <span style="background-color: #FFFFFF">
                     October
                    </span>
                   </font>
                   , our partisan 
weighting targets are
                   <font color="#0000FF">
                    37.0% Democrat
                   </font>
                   ,
                   <font color="#FF0000">
                    32.3% Republican
                   </font>
                   , and 30.7% unaffiliated. That's 
little changed from our September targets of 37.0% Democrat, 32.7% Republican, 
and 30.2% unaffiliated
                   <span style="color:black">
                    .
                   </span>
                  </i>
                  <span style="color: black">
                   In an October 12 posting on his website, Rasmussen reports his polls have 
detected "no 
impact" of the scandal involving former Congressperson Mark Foley on party 
ID. On
                  </span>
                  <font color="#008000">
                   November 1
                  </font>
                  <span style="color: black">
                   , 
Rasmussen announced
new party ID figures of
                  </span>
                  <font color="#0000FF">
                   37.7% D
                  </font>
                  and
                  <font color="#FF0000">
                   31.5% R
                  </font>
                  . Since there's not that much time 
until the elections, I'll just keep the October figures at the top of the chart.
                 </font>
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 <font size="2">
                  (
                  <font color="#FF0000">

                  </font>
                  ) For registered and likely voters, respectively.
                 </font>
                </b>
               </p>
               <p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: left">
                <font size="2">

                  (
                  <font color="#FF0000">

                  </font>

                 </font>

               <hr/>
               <p align="center">
                <b>
                 <font size="5">
                  <a name="essay">
                  </a>
                  <br/>
                  Weighting Pre-Election Polls for Party
Composition:
                  <br/>
                  Should Pollsters Do It or Not?
                 </font>
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="center">
                <font color="#FF0000">
                 <b>
                  by Alan Reifman
                 </b>
                </font>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 <font color="#FF0000">
                  September 9, 2004 --
                 </font>
                 We have seen a lot of polls thus far in
the Bush-Kerry race and we're going to see a lot more. Often polls by two
different survey outfits taken at the
                 <i>
                  same time
                 </i>
                 will show results in
pretty stark disagreement. Literally as I write this, Rasmussen
Reports has the race a virtual dead-heat (Bush 47.5, Kerry 46.8; link no longer 
available), while
                 <a href="http://www.cbsnews.com/stories/2004/09/09/opinion/polls/main642108.shtml" target="_blank">
                  CBS
News
                 </a>
                 has Bush up by 7.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 All pollsters try to obtain a random, representative sample
of voters to represent the full electorate. In addition to vote choice
(i.e., Bush, Kerry, or other), pollsters always ask respondents which party they
align themselves with. These two measures -- candidate preference and
party ID -- often show great overlap, with Republicans (R's) heavily going for
Bush and Democrats (D's) heavily going for Kerry. However, people
sometimes vote for the other party's candidate, so candidate preference and
party ID are not identical.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 One factor (among many) that may contribute to discrepancies
between different outfits' polls in their Bush-Kerry margins, I will argue, is polling
firms' different philosophies as to whether it's advisable to mathematically
adjust their samples -- after all the interviews have been completed -- to make
the percentages of D's and R's in their survey sample match the partisan
composition that is likely to be evident at the polls on Election Day. The
latter can be estimated from exit polls from previous elections, party
registration figures (in states where citizens declare a party ID when
registering to vote), and surveys.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 (Another issue that often comes up in evaluating pre-election
surveys, with which many of you may be familiar, is whether results are reported
for "registered" or "likely" voters. That is a
different issue from what is being discussed presently. Whether a pollster
reports results for registered voters, likely voters, or both, weighting by
party ID is a separate, independent decision.)
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 Exit polls from the three
previous presidential elections yield the following percentages of the
electorate comprised of self-identified D's, R's, and independents (from Zogby; 
link no longer available).
                </b>
               </p>
               <div align="left">
                <table align="center" class="style46" style="width: 85%">
                 <tr>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#00FF00" width="17%">
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#00FFFF" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    Democrats
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FF99CC" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    Republicans
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FFFF00" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    Independents
                   </b>
                  </td>
                 </tr>
                 <tr>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#00FF00" width="17%">
                   <b>
                    1992
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#00FFFF" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    34%
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FF99CC" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    34%
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FFFF00" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    33%
                   </b>
                  </td>
                 </tr>
                 <tr>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#00FF00" width="17%">
                   <b>
                    1996
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#00FFFF" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    39%
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FF99CC" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    34%
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FFFF00" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    27%
                   </b>
                  </td>
                 </tr>
                 <tr>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#00FF00" width="17%">
                   <b>
                    2000
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#00FFFF" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    39%
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FF99CC" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    35%
                   </b>
                  </td>
                  <td align="center" bgcolor="#FFFF00" width="30%">
                   <b>
                    26%
                   </b>
                  </td>
                 </tr>
                </table>
               </div>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 One additional
source is Democratic pollster Stanley Greenberg, author of
                 <i>
                  The
Two Americas
                 </i>
                 . He found, after conducting 15 national polls with an
aggregate 15,045 voters from late 2001 until early 2003 and allocating "leaners"
to the relevant party, that each major party had the allegiance of 46% of the
voters.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 The controversy occurs when a poll of, say, 1,000 voters
shows a partisan composition vastly different from what we've come to
expect. Should the pollster make statistical adjustments (described below)
to make the party breakdown conform to more typical estimates, or should he/she
just leave the numbers alone and report the findings?
                </b>
                <b>
                 A good
summary of the "back and forth" of this
controversy is available in this
                 <em>
                  Los Angeles Times
                 </em>
                 <a href="http://articles.latimes.com/2004/jun/22/nation/na-polling22" target="_blank">
                  article
                 </a>
                 from earlier this summer. I will be referring back to this article.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 The following two scenarios should illustrate the key
issues. Before presenting the scenarios, I want to state that there are
noted national authorities on either side of the "weight/no weight"
debate (including Democrats on both sides and Republicans on
both sides). Each reader should decide for him- or herself. A simple 
illustration of how to
actually implement a sample weighting is described in Appendix A, with 
references for more complex situations in Appendix B.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 <font color="#FF3300">
                  SCENARIO 1
                 </font>
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 As noted above, most recent estimates of the partisan
composition of the electorate suggest a rough balance between the number of
voters leaning toward the D and R parties (i.e., "50/50 nation"), with
the possibility that there might be slightly more D's than R's.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 In his aforementioned book, Greenberg characterizes party
identification as "... a form of social identity, not unlike ethnicity or
race, with considerable durability over time" (p. 93). I would argue
that individual-level stability should
                 <i>
                  generally
                 </i>
                 lead to population-level
stability, although not perfectly so (e.g., from one presidential election to
the next, some people pass away, others newly turn 18, immigrants become
eligible to vote).
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 Suppose a pollster completes a survey and finds far more
self-identified R's in the sample than D's. This happened in the
                 <i>
                  Newsweek
                 </i>
                 poll released in early September right after the R convention that gave Bush an
11-point lead over Kerry.
                 <i>
                  Newsweek
                 </i>
                 's sample contained
                 <span class="style10">
                  38% R
                 </span>
                 ,
                 <span class="style57">
                  31% D
                 </span>
                 , and
                 <span class="style58">
                  31% I
                 </span>
                 (article links no longer available).
There would seem to be three plausible explanations for the higher-than-usual
sampling of R's:
                </b>
               </p>
               <ul>
                <li>
                 <p align="left">
                  <b>
                   There was a sudden, massive shift in party ID after the R
    convention.
                  </b>
                 </p>
                </li>
                <li>
                 <p align="left">
                  <b>
                   Given that
                   <i>
                    Newsweek
                   </i>
                   's polling was done on Sept.
    2-3 (partially overlapping the convention), one could argue that more R's
    than D's would have made it a point to be home to watch the convention, thus
    making themselves more accessible to telephone interviewers; even
    after the final day of the convention, R's may have been more politically
    energized, making them more likely to agree to participate in a survey.
                  </b>
                 </p>
                </li>
                <li>
                 <p align="left">
                  <b>
                   It could have just been plain, "old fashioned"
    sampling error -- just as a coin, with probabilities of 50% heads and 50%
    tails, can yield 60% heads in a sequence of flips, random sampling of
    households could have yielded excessive R's just by chance.
                  </b>
                 </p>
                </li>
               </ul>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 If Greenberg is correct about the stability of individuals'
party ID, then the first of the three explanations (a sudden shift) seems
unlikely. The fact that other recent polls besides
                 <i>
                  Newsweek's
                 </i>
                 have
obtained samples with more R's than D's seems to go against the third
explanation (chance). In any event, we would conclude that if the second or
third explanation were the true "culprit,"
                 <i>
                  Newsweek's
                 </i>
                 party
breakdown would appear to be "out of
whack" relative to the other aforementioned indicators.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 (Again, to keep things bipartisan, the article I cited
earlier as providing a good discussion of the "back and forth" of the
controversy was itself prompted by a mid-summer
                 <i>
                  L.A. Times
                 </i>
                 poll in which
it appeared there were way too many Democrats in the sample.)
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 It is at this point that pollsters face the choice of whether
to adjust the numbers to match more typical estimates of the D-R distribution
(i.e., count R's less and D's more), or just leave the sample alone.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 In 2000, pollster Scott Rasmussen went with the "leave
things alone" strategy, with the result that his firm forecast a 9-point
Bush victory over Gore in its final pre-election poll. Rasmussen, to his
credit, posted a candid summary on his website:
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 "Simply put, we had too many Republicans in our
sample. For a variety of reasons, our firm has never weighted by
party. However, if we had weighted the data before the election to include
an equal number of Republicans and Democrats, we would have shown Bush leading
by 2 points. Had we weighted our data to match the partisan mix reported
by the Voter News Service on Election Night, we would have shown Gore leading by
a point"(document no longer available
online).
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 One would surmise that Rasmussen is probably weighting this
year.
                 <i>
                  [As shown above on this website in connection with the 2008 
presidential election,Rasmussen 
indeed adopted a practice of weighting by party ID.]
                 </i>
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 Pollster John Zogby has pioneered the art of sample weighting
on party ID. Taking 1996 and 2000 together, he was
                 <i>
                  the
                 </i>
                 most
accurate pollster in forecasting the two presidential elections. As he
noted on
his website, "My polls use a party weight of 39% Democrat, 35% Republican
and 26% Independent"
                 <em>
                  [this 2004 document is no longer online].
                 </em>
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <b>
                 Another apparent sign of polls that weight is that they
should exhibit less volatility day-to-day or week-to-week than polls that don't
weight.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p align="left">
                <font color="#FF3300">
                 <b>
                  SCENARIO 2
                 </b>
                </font>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 As previewed above, a number of prominent polling authorities would
presumably argue that the
                 <i>
                  Newsweek
                 </i>
                 poll, with its larger-than-typical R
composition, or the
                 <i>
                  L.A. Times
                 </i>
                 poll, with the unusually wide D edge,
should be left alone and not "retrofitted" into some preconceived
template of what the Election Day partisan composition will look like.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 According to the
                 <em>
                  L. A. Times
                 </em>
                 "back and forth" article cited above:
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 "Andrew Kohut of the
                 <a href="http://people-press.org/" target="_blank">
                  Pew Research
Center for the People and the Press
                 </a>
                 said that he once conducted a survey
asking voters their party twice, four days apart, and that he found substantial
differences in the responses."
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 Further, even though Democratic candidates sometimes come out with more
favorable readings on party-weighted compared to unweighted polls, Ruy Teixeira,
co-author of
                 <i>
                  The Emerging Democratic Majority
                 </i>
                 and operator of a "blog"
related to the book [moved to
                 <a href="http://www.thedemocraticstrategist.org/" target="_blank">
                  here
                 </a>
                 ], opposes weighting for party ID. In his September 5
entry, he writes:
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 "Does that mean I favor polls like this weighting their samples by
party ID? No, I don't, because the distribution of party ID does shift some over
time and polls should be able to capture this. What I do favor is release and
prominent display of sample compostions [
                 <i>
                  sic
                 </i>
                 ] by party ID, as well as
basic demographics, whenever a poll comes out. Consumers of poll data should not
have to ferret out this information from obscure places--it should be given
out-front by the polling organizations or sponsors themselves. Then people can
use this information to make judgements [
                 <i>
                  sic
                 </i>
                 ] about whether and to what
extent they find the results of the poll plausible."
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 If I had to argue for
                 <i>
                  not
                 </i>
                 weighting on party ID, I would make two
points:
                </b>
               </p>
               <ul>
                <li>
                 <p style="text-align: left">
                  <b>
                   As embodied in Teixeira's quote, a "free market of ideas"
    should prevail. With as much data as possible being released with
    polls, consumers can reach their own conclusions. And, of course,
    pollsters acquire reputations over the years as to their surveys' accuracy
    in forecasting elections. Over the long run, this should serve as a
    check and balance on polling/statistical procedures that have led them
    astray.
                  </b>
                 </p>
                </li>
                <li>
                 <p style="text-align: left">
                  <b>
                   Some pollsters who do not weight on party ID may weight on other
    demographic characteristics (e.g., sex, race/ethnicity), on the grounds that
    whether one is male or female is far more stable than whether one is a D or
    R. This may help eliminate some of the skew when people appear to be
    represented disproportionately.
                  </b>
                 </p>
                </li>
               </ul>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 Hopefully my essay has given you something to think about as the
pre-election polls roll in. There are no definitive answers on how to
handle these issues. For all we know, Election Day 2004 may have more R's
voting than D's, a departure from the last three presidential elections.
In that event, polls not weighted on party ID may end up being more accurate
than those with weightings based on recent past presidential elections.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 My personal advice -- whether, to use a phrase
from Senator John McCain, you're a "Republican, Democrat, Libertarian or
vegetarian" -- is to interpret polls favorable to your chosen candidate in
cautious terms. If the favorable poll turns out to be accurate on Election
Day, you can exult, but if it was flawed, at least you won't be blindsided.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p>
                <i>
                 <b>
                  I would like to thank John Welte for discussing sample weighting with
me over the years.
                 </b>
                </i>
               </p>
               <p align="center">
                <b>
                 Click
                 <a href="http://courses.ttu.edu/hdfs3390-reifman/weighting.htm">
                  here
                 </a>
                 to return to top 
of page.
                </b>
               </p>
               <hr/>
               <p align="center">
                <a name="postscript04">
                </a>
               </p>
               <table align="center" class="style17" id="table8" width="70%">
                <tr>
                 <td align="center" bgcolor="" class="style45" width="70%">
                  <font color="#FFFF00" size="4">
                   <b>
                    Postscript
        on Sample Weighting in the 2004 Presidential Election
                   </b>
                  </font>
                  <p align="left">
                   <b>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     November 9, 2004 --
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     Here are some of my 
		initial post-election thoughts on the sample-weighting issue. As 
		more information becomes available, I may refine my opinions. Given that 
		(a) polls over the campaign's final months with partisan composition 
		weighted to previous turnouts tended to show Kerry doing better than 
		polls without such weighting and (b) Kerry lost, one's immediate 
		reaction might be that party ID weighting should now be abandoned (at 
		least tentatively). However, the available evidence does
                    </font>
                    <i>
                     <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                      not
                     </font>
                    </i>
                    <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     strongly support that view. Bush won the national popular vote
        51%-48%. Zogby, who we know weights on party ID, had it
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" style="font-size: 11pt">
                    </font>
                    <font color="#000099">
                     <a href="http://www.mysterypollster.com/main/2004/11/first_impressio.html" target="_blank">
                      <font color="#00FFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                       Bush
        48-47
                      </font>
                     </a>
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" style="font-size: 11pt">
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     in his final national poll, not too far away. Rasmussen,
        who is believed to weight (see below), had it
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" style="font-size: 11pt">
                    </font>
                    <font color="#000099">
                     <a href="http://www.rasmussenreports.com/Presidential_Tracking_Poll.htm">
                      <font color="#00FFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                       Bush
        50.2-48.5
                      </font>
                     </a>
                     <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                      .
        Other firms such as Gallup (whose final likely voter poll had it Bush
        49-47 before allocation of undecideds) that don't weight on party ID
        also came pretty close to the actual figure, but the point is firms
        that (presumably) weighted on party ID wre not led heavily
        astray. A central issue, of course, is what the
                     </font>
                    </font>
                    <i>
                     <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                      actual
                     </font>
                    </i>
                    <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     partiorate is on Election Day.
        According to a national
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" style="font-size: 11pt">
                    </font>
                    <font color="#000099">
                     <a href="http://www.cnn.com/ELECTION/2004/pages/results/states/US/P/00/epolls.0.html" target="_blank">
                      <font color="#00FFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                       exit
        poll
                      </font>
                     </a>
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" style="font-size: 11pt">
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     of 13,660 respondents posted by CNN, this year's electorate was
        comprised of 37% Democrats, 37% Republicans, and 26% Independents.
        Compared to 2000 figures (shown below), this would represent a 2%
        reduction in Democrats and a corresponding 2% gain in Republicans, with
        the percent of Independents the same. The relatively small size of
        the shift presumably would
        explain why weighting polls this year on 2000 turnout did not cause major damage.
        (We know that the 2004 exit polls were messed up; if they were accurate,
        Kerry would now be selecting his Cabinet. The exit poll posted by
        CNN apparently included adjustments to make the overall Bush-Kerry
        percentages match the actual vote. Given these considerations, I
        suspect there's still some degree of uncertainty in the 2004
        partisan-composition estimates.) Assuming the 37-37 D-R voter
        composition to be roughly accurate, however, I think we can safely say
        that polls from this past election season showing substantial GOP edges
        in sample representation (
                    </font>
                    <font color="#000099">
                     <font color="#00FFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                      <a href="http://www.theleftcoaster.com/archives/002904.php" target="_blank">
                       <font color="#00FFFF">
                        one
        Gallup poll
                       </font>
                      </a>
                     </font>
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     , 
		taken from September 24-26, had
                     <i>
                      12%
                     </i>
                     more R's than D's!) were clearly out of
        bounds.
                    </font>
                   </b>
                  </p>
                  <p align="left">
                   <b>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     ADDENDUM (11/17/04):
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     A
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" style="font-size: 11pt">
                    </font>
                    <font color="#000099">
                     <a href="http://pollingreport.com/2004.htm#Exit" target="_blank">
                      <font color="#00FFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                       Los Angeles Times
        national exit poll
                      </font>
                     </a>
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" style="font-size: 11pt">
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFFFF" style="font-size: 11pt">
                     with 5,154 respondents came up with the following
        percentages of the electorate: 40 D, 39 R, and 19 I. This
        matches fairly well with the figures from the other exit poll noted
        above. (If you go to the linked document for the L.A. Times exit
        poll, you'll notice the high number of Californians surveyed; they were
        presumably weighted down to match California's share of the national
        population.)
                    </font>
                    <font color="#FFFF00" size="2">
                     <br/>
                    </font>
                   </b>
                  </p>
                 </td>

               <p>
                <b>
                 <font color="#FF3300">
                  <a name="appendix">
                  </a>
                  <br/>
                  <span class="style47">
                   APPENDIX A:
                  </span>
                 </font>
                 <span class="style47">
                  Implementing Sample Weights in the
                 </span>
                 <a href="http://www.spss.com/" target="_blank">
                  <span class="style47">
                   SPSS
                  </span>
                 </a>
                 <span class="style47">
                  Program
                 </span>
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 As shown in the grid below, I have created a fictitious data set of 30
respondents. I made it so that 10 Democrats were sampled (80% of them
voting for Kerry, in accordance with actual survey estimates), along with 20
Republicans (with 90% for Bush). The gray shading is simply meant to break
the grid into blocks of five lines, making it easier to count cases.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 If no weighting by party were done, Bush would be leading 66%-33% (20 to
10 in raw numbers).
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 If we want to re-estimate the sample with 50% Republicans and 50%
Democrats (which roughly matches some actual estimates), then we would weight
each Democrat 1.50 (to bring them from 10 respondents to 15) and weight each
Republican .75 (to bring them from 20 respondents to 15).
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 The weights should be treated like any other variable, with a name such as
"pweight" for party weight. In SPSS, you would go to the
"Data" menu, then "Weight Cases," then weight by pweight.
                </b>
               </p>
               <p style="text-align: left">
                <b>
                 With the weighting implemented, the sample becomes equalized on numbers of
D's and R's, and Bush's lead shrinks to 55%-45%. (The easiest way to
visualize this is to aggregate the weights of the original Kerry voters [8 X
1.50] + [2 X .75] = 13.50, which is then divided by 30, yielding .45.)
                </b>
               </p>
               <div align="left">
                <table border="1" height="794" width="100%">
                 <tr>
                  <td align="center" height="38" width="33%">
                   <font size="3">
                    <b>

                    </b>
                   </font>

                    <b>

                     <br/>
                     (1 = Kerry, 2 = Bush)
                    </b>
                   </font>

                     <br/>
                     (pweight)
                    </b>
                   </font>
                  </td>
